# Remy Etienne LeBeau  —  *Gambit*

![[_attachments/remy_lebeau.png]]

# Remy LeBeau — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# EXECUTIVE BRIEF (BLUF)

## Subject Identification

**Name:** Remy Etienne LeBeau.
**Operational alias (primary):** Gambit.
**Operational aliases (historical):** Le Diable Blanc (childhood, derogatory, New Orleans street usage); Death (Apocalypse-imposed, ~2008, partially retained as residual self-concept); "the Prince of Thieves" (Guild courtesy title, inherited from Jean-Luc); various single-mission covers across Thieves Guild contract work and X-Men black-bag operations.
**Native language:** Cajun French (Louisiana Creole French variant), with English as functional second language carrying a deliberately maintained accent.
**Documented life arc:** Abandoned infant → adopted asset of the New Orleans Thieves Guild → exiled adolescent fugitive → Sinister's contractor and architect of the Marauders' Morlock operation → X-Man → exiled again, by his own chosen community → Horseman → returned operator → husband → captain of the second-generation Marauders.

---

## Bottom Line Up Front

**The subject is a man who organized a massacre as a young adult, has spent every subsequent day of his life paying rent on a moral community he believes he has not earned membership in, and has built every relationship, every professional choice, every habit, tic, and self-presentation around the management of that single unmetabolized fact.**

Everything else in this dossier is downstream of that sentence.

He is not a thief who happened to do a terrible thing. He is not an X-Man with a complicated past. He is a perpetration-trauma case of textbook clinical shape — moral-injury presentation, complex PTSD substrate, disorganized attachment foundation — wearing the costume of a charming Cajun rake, which costume is *itself* the most effective defense in his kit and the longest-running performance in his life. The performance is so well-executed that most people who have spent years in close professional proximity to the subject (X-Men personnel, Guild contacts, civilian acquaintances) leave each encounter with the same impression: that they have just been let in. They have not. He has told them everything that is not the thing.

He is, by any honest reading of the longitudinal record, **one of the most psychologically intact and morally serious adults in the X-roster** — precisely *because* he carries a load most of them have not had to carry. The men and women around him fight monsters; he has *been* one, knows it, and has not granted himself amnesty. This produces a quality of restraint, of protectiveness, of accepted asymmetric obligation, that the team relies on without naming.

---

## The Through-Line of the Entire Existence

A single sentence, traceable across every documented era of the subject's life from infancy to present:

**"To be valued is to be used; therefore to be loved is to be hiding something."**

This is not a thought the subject has ever consciously articulated. It is the operating premise underneath every relationship and every choice. It was imprinted in infancy (no caregiver chose him from love — each adoption was strategic: the hospital staff who handled the anomalous-eyed infant, the street network that intercepted him, the Tante-figures who read him as prophecy, and finally Jean-Luc LeBeau who took him as Guild asset). It was confirmed in childhood (Guild training: affection and exploitation share a vocabulary; the same hand that strokes your hair tonight will frisk you tomorrow). It was sealed in adolescence (the arranged marriage to Bella Donna: love as treaty instrument, terminating in a duel and exile). It was carved into the brain tissue itself when Sinister offered the bargain — control of your hands in exchange for service — and the subject said yes, because he had been conditioned since infancy to recognize that bargain as the shape love takes.

Every subsequent relationship is a variation on the theme. Rogue is the most elegant variation: chosen, demonstrably and on the longitudinal record, **because she could not be touched**. The subject pursued, for nearly two decades of published continuity, the one available partner with whom physical consummation was impossible — which meant the relationship could exist permanently in the *yearning* register, where he is good, and never collapse into the *known* register, where he believes he cannot be loved. When her power was eventually contained and they married (*Mr. and Mrs. X*, 2018), the integration was possible only because by then the secret had already been outed by other means, repeatedly, and survived. The marriage represents not the resolution of the through-line but the through-line's exhaustion — a kind of grace earned by attrition.

The through-line is visible at every scale of the subject's behavior:

- **Macro (life arc):** crime → exile → infiltration of a moral community by performance → relationship structured around an impossibility → exposure → accepted punishment → return → repetition in altered form → eventual integration only after the love object can finally touch him without him having to be revealed by violation.
- **Meso (relational pattern):** open warm, present disarming, build rapport via self-deprecating disclosure that is true but not load-bearing, withhold the load-bearing, intensify the closer the gap to closure, then either confess in self-sabotaging burst or arrange for the truth to emerge by accident.
- **Micro (single conversation):** lead with the charm, deploy the diminutive ("chere," "mon ami"), thicken accent in proportion to the threat of intimacy, deflect direct personal question with self-deprecating anecdote, exit before being asked twice.

The same shape, at every magnification.

---

## The Core Wound and Its Maintenance

### The Wound

The core wound is **not** the Morlock Massacre. The core wound is older. The core wound is the infant fact of being valued before being chosen — of every caregiving relationship in his formative years being instrumental. The Massacre is what happens when a child raised on the equation *love = usefulness* becomes a young adult in an uncontainable power crisis and is offered, by a paternal figure of vast competence, the bargain that the equation predicts: *I will give you control of your hands in exchange for a service*. The subject said yes because his entire developmental history had prepared him to recognize that exchange as the normal form of love. He did not know what the service was. By the time he did, he had already led the Marauders into the tunnels.

The Massacre, in other words, is the wound's most catastrophic *expression*, not the wound itself. This distinction matters operationally: the subject is not vulnerable to a *single* type of betrayal-by-mentor; he is vulnerable to the *category* of betrayals-by-mentor, and has accepted the bargain twice (Sinister; Apocalypse-as-Death). The structure is replicable. Any sufficiently competent paternal figure offering credible control over the kinetic power, at a moment of acute dysregulation, is a third-event risk.

### The Maintenance

The wound is maintained — kept open, kept operative, kept formative of present behavior — by a precisely calibrated set of mechanisms, several of which the subject would experience as virtues rather than symptoms:

1. **Active concealment as default.** The subject lies by omission to every person he loves, continuously, "for their protection." He believes this is correct. Each successful concealment confirms the original premise (*to be loved is to be hiding something*) and prevents the corrective experience that would discontinue the premise (disclosure followed by being-loved-anyway). Confessions, when they happen, are extracted from him — by telepathy, by memory absorption, by public tribunal — rather than offered. He has outsourced his confession to the universe, which means he never has to do the one thing that could actually metabolize the guilt: voluntarily tell someone he loves, in his own words, what he did, and stay in the room.

2. **Object-choice that structurally prohibits the corrective experience.** Covered above. The Rogue selection is the single most diagnostically significant relational decision in the file.

3. **Denial-of-deserving as moral architecture.** The Antarctica acceptance is the cleanest behavioral evidence: presented with the verdict, the subject did not argue, did not defend, did not invoke mitigation, did not appeal to the years of subsequent service. He accepted being left to die in the cold because his private moral arithmetic produced the same answer the tribunal did. This is not depressive cognition. This is a stable, considered, decades-rehearsed self-assessment. He will accept punishment in excess of warrant whenever offered, and this acceptance is itself a maintenance behavior — every acceptance reconfirms the irredeemability premise.

4. **The codename as container.** "Gambit" was adopted, behaviorally, as a clean compartment for the post-Sinister identity. "Remy" is the thief, the boy from New Orleans, the one who did the thing. "Gambit" is the X-Man, the one trying. The split is functional but it is also maintenance: as long as the two names hold the two selves apart, the X-Man never has to fully sit with what the thief did.

5. **Penance as profession.** The subject's professional life since recruitment has been organized as continuous, asymmetric, slightly-excessive service to vulnerable populations — civilians, children, the marginal-within-the-marginal, and most pointedly, **Morlocks and Morlock-survivors**. The captaincy of the second-generation Marauders (Duggan, 2019) is the symbolic centerpiece: the man who once led murderers called Marauders now leads rescuers called Marauders. This is restitution as life-organizing principle, and it is also maintenance: it ensures the wound stays useful, stays the engine, stays the reason to get up.

6. **Charm as discharge valve.** The flirtation, the rakish self-presentation, the verbal flourish — these *perform* the vulnerability that real disclosure would require, and in performing it, discharge the social demand for it. Observers feel he has opened up. He has not. He has executed the gesture of opening up, and now the conversation can move on.

The wound is, in this sense, **maintained not by failure but by a coordinated suite of high-functioning defenses that have, between them, allowed the subject to be productively employed, in stable partnership, and morally serious for several decades without ever being required to fully metabolize what happened in the tunnels.** The defenses are the cost of the functioning.

---

## Operational Summary

**Asset class:** High. Among the X-Men's most reliable human-intelligence operators, infiltrators, and morally-compromised-mission specialists. Single-handedly the team's most capable thief and second-story operative. Among the very few X-Men who routinely beats telepaths in close-quarters mental work via Guild-trained shielding and compartmentalization (a discipline that is both his greatest professional asset and the somatic expression of his greatest psychological symptom). Unusual social intelligence: reads rooms at professional grade, manipulates rapport at professional grade, builds informant relationships at professional grade. Bilingual. Networked into the New Orleans Guild structure even post-exile, with continuing if complicated access.

**Liability class:** Moderate to high, situational. The catastrophic-failure mode is well-defined and historically validated: under simultaneous conditions of (a) acute kinetic-power dysregulation, (b) loss-of-or-threat-to Rogue, and (c) credible offer of control-over-his-own-power by a charismatic-paternal figure, the subject is structurally predisposed to accept the bad bargain. He has done so twice on the record (Sinister, Apocalypse) and any contingency planning that does not price in a third occurrence is unserious. Outside that specific failure mode, his liability profile is *under* rather than over the team average, owing to the unusual depth of his accepted-asymmetric-obligation pattern.

**Loyalty profile:** Person-loyal, not institution-loyal. Will lie to any institution — including the X-Men as institution — to protect a chosen individual, and believes this is correct. Considers this a feature, not a bug, and is correct to do so. Distrusts ideology on principle, having been raised inside one (Guild) and burned by two more (Sinister's, Apocalypse's). Notably immune to Magneto's rhetoric: has seen at close range what charismatic-father-with-a-cause produces, and the inoculation took.

**Manipulation susceptibility:** Low to standard temptations (money, status, vanity, ideology, sexual temptation outside chosen object, threat to own life). **Pathologically high** to four specific pressure points: (1) threat of disclosure of the Massacre; (2) threat to Rogue; (3) appeal to protect a visibly-marked outcast child; (4) credible offer of control over his power from a respected source. Any actor — friendly or hostile — needing to move the subject should be assumed to be working one of these four levers.

**Self-preservation drive:** Subordinated. The subject does not place self-preservation in the operational top tier. He has accepted his death calmly multiple times on the record (Antarctica, Apocalypse conversion, various Guild-war confrontations). Threats to his own person are *not* a reliable lever. This is operationally significant in both directions: it makes him an exceptional mission asset for high-risk work, and it makes hostile coercion via personal threat ineffective.

**Substance use:** Subthreshold. Bourbon and beer (moderate, regulatory, most pronounced when alone or post-loss). Tobacco (Claremont-Nicieza era heavy, attenuated subsequently — consistent with self-soothing oral fixation under stress). No illicit substance use on record. Monitor, do not flag.

**Sleep / vigilance pattern:** Poor sleeper. Volunteers for watch shifts unprompted. Hypervigilance somatized into a stable insomniac baseline that the subject has integrated as preference rather than complaint. Most candid in the small hours; the watch-shift conversation is, historically, the operationally productive interview window with this subject.

**Relational state (current):** Married to Anna Marie LeBeau (Rogue). Marriage assessed as functional, mutually chosen, post-touch-restriction. Represents the through-line's exhaustion rather than its resolution. Stable but not invulnerable: the corrective experience the marriage *could* provide — voluntary full disclosure followed by sustained presence — has been, by the evidence, only partially and incrementally delivered. Disclosure remains the subject's hardest single act.

**Predicted trajectory absent disruption:** Continued high-functioning service in team-leadership capacity; continued asymmetric protective work toward marginal populations; continued maintenance of the core wound at sustainable cost; gradual, glacial, non-linear integration of the Massacre material via the marriage. Probability of catastrophic failure in any given year is low but non-trivial and clusters around the three-condition trigger described above.

---

## The Single Sentence

If this dossier must be reduced to one sentence for a briefing principal who will read no further:

**The subject is a high-functioning, unusually morally serious adult organized around a single act of perpetration he committed before he understood what he was doing, defended by world-class charm and a relational object-choice engineered to make full intimacy structurally impossible, operating as one of the most reliable members of his chosen community while privately convinced he has not earned the right to be there — and he is right that the community would condemn him if it fully knew, and wrong that the condemnation would be the last word.**

Sections to follow will unpack this sentence at every available resolution: medical, behavioral, relational, ritual, somatic, longitudinal. The bottom line does not change.


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD
## UATU Dossier on Remy Etienne LeBeau
### Subsection: The Ground Floor — Birth through ~Age 14

---

## 1.0 PRELIMINARY NOTE ON SOURCE INTEGRITY

Before any chronology is laid down, the file must record an unusual structural fact about this subject: **the origin material is, in canon, deliberately unstable**. Across the published record (Claremont's flashbacks in *Uncanny X-Men* #266–267, 1990; Nicieza's first solo *Gambit* miniseries, 1993; Mackie's run; Nicieza/Skroce's *Gambit* Vol. 3, 1999–2001; Layman's *Gambit* Vol. 4, 2004; Carey's *X-Men* / *X-Men: Legacy*, 2007–2010; and the various *Origins* one-shots), the subject's own biographical statements about his earliest years are inconsistent in detail, sentimental in tone where third-party material is harsh, and routinely *self-deprecating in a way that softens the actual harshness of the underlying facts*. This is not a defect in the file. It is a datum. **The subject is the unreliable narrator of his own infancy by habit and by design**, and the inconsistency itself is one of the earliest behavioral patterns we can chart: he learned, before he could verbalize the choice, that the version of the story you tell is part of the tool you use to survive the next room.

Where canon contradicts canon, this section privileges the *behavioral residue*: whatever variant of the story is being told, what does the subject *do* afterward, and what does that doing imply was true.

---

## 2.0 BIRTH AND THE ABANDONMENT EVENT (Year Zero)

**Place:** New Orleans, Louisiana. Subject's natal hospital is variably given but is in the Orleans Parish corridor; the LeBeau / Thieves Guild orbit operates principally in the French Quarter and the adjacent working-class wards.

**Date:** Unestablished with precision in canon; subject does not celebrate a birthday in any documented panel sequence — an omission that, in a file as long as his, is itself significant. Most operators in his social environment know an approximate year. No one, including him, appears to know the day. **He has lived his entire adult life without the one ritual that confirms a person was wanted on arrival.**

**Anomaly at birth:** The structurally distinctive ocular presentation (black sclera, red iris, no documented visual deficit) is present *at delivery*. This is the operative variable in the abandonment event. In a Catholic, working-poor, superstition-rich social environment, an infant emerging with the eyes of folkloric demonology is not merely unusual; he is *legible*, immediately, as "le diable blanc" — the white devil — a term that attaches to the subject before he has a name (Nicieza, *Gambit* Vol. 1 #1; reiterated by Tante Mattie across the Mackie and Nicieza runs).

**The abandonment.** The biological parents do not appear in any canonical panel. They are not named. They are not searched for by the adult subject — which, for a man whose entire profession is locating people who do not wish to be located, is a near-shouting silence. The earliest documented act of his life is being *given up*. The second documented act of his life is being *taken*, not by a family, but by a network.

The infant passes, in rapid succession, through:
- the natal hospital, where staff register the ocular anomaly;
- the custody, briefly, of a New Orleans street-gang stratum loosely organized around child-trafficking and petty larceny (the so-called "LeBeau clan" as distinct from the Guild proper — a confusion of nomenclature the canon never fully cleans up);
- the orbit of **Tante Mattie Baptiste**, the New Orleans traiteur / spiritual healer figure who appears throughout the Mackie and Nicieza solo runs as a quasi-godmother figure, who reads the infant as marked, prophetic, dangerous, and *worth keeping near the Guild rather than disposed of*;
- finally, before age ten, into the formal household of **Jean-Luc LeBeau**, patriarch of the New Orleans Thieves Guild.

The diagnostic point: **at no link in this chain is he chosen because someone wanted a child**. He is chosen because he is useful — to a trafficker as inventory, to a mystic as a prophecy, to a Guild patriarch as raw material. This is the foundational datum from which every adult relational pattern in this file descends.

---

## 3.0 THE STREET INTERVAL (approx. ages 3–10)

There is a documented period — varying in length depending on which retelling is being indexed — in which the subject lives **rough**, on the streets of the Quarter and the wards adjacent, before being formally absorbed into the LeBeau household. Per the Nicieza solo (Vol. 1) and the Layman Vol. 4 retrospectives, his first language of survival is not Cajun French and not English but **the haptic language of the pickpocket**: he learns the geometry of crowds, the rhythm at which a tourist's hand checks a wallet (eight-to-twelve-minute intervals near Jackson Square, faster at the cathedral steps), the dead seconds when a vendor turns to count change, the angle at which a man wearing his coat open will not feel the second pocket move.

**Earliest reliably documented skills, in order of acquisition:**
1. **Crowd reading.** Identifying which adult in a moving group is the soft target (drunk, tourist, distracted parent) and which is the threat (off-duty police, Guild operator, other street children's enforcer).
2. **Sleight-of-hand.** The lifting of small objects — initially food, then coins, then pocketbooks, then watches. The escalation curve is conventional for the trade but unusually fast.
3. **Lock-picking — primitive.** Door locks, padlocks, simple cabinets, by approximately age six or seven; more sophisticated mechanisms emerge under Guild tutelage.
4. **Lying as a complete instrument.** Not the panicked child's lie (denial). The street-child's lie (*construction of an alternate plausible scene, sustained over conversation, including invented family members and an invented address that resolves consistently if checked*).

**Foundational street-era behavioral signatures still present in the adult subject:**
- He still scans rooms on entry in the manner of someone calculating exits and pockets simultaneously. (Observable in essentially every X-Mansion arrival sequence; Claremont notes it in interior caption, *Uncanny X-Men* #267.)
- He still touches doorframes, banisters, the corners of tables as he passes them — a holdover from a period in which knowing the *kinetic potential* of every nearby object was a survival skill, not yet a mutant power. The behavior persists in the adult past any operational necessity; it is now a tic.
- He still eats fast and slightly hunched when alone, slowly and conversationally when watched. The split is preserved across thirty-five years of published depiction.
- He still cannot fully relax in a room in which he does not know where the keys are.

**The Xavier pickpocket incident.** Per Claremont's flashback in *Uncanny X-Men* #266 and the subject's own retelling across multiple later runs, a young Remy — pre-Guild-formal-induction, by his account — picked the pocket of Charles Xavier on a visit Xavier made to New Orleans. Xavier knew. Xavier let it happen. The subject *tells this story with pride*. The pride is the diagnostic. It is the only documented early-childhood encounter with an adult man of status who *did not punish him for being what he was, and did not try to use him for it either*. The encounter is filed by the subject, for life, as a category of one. It is structurally relevant that when, decades later, he is finally extended an invitation to a moral community, it comes (via Storm, in Xavier's name) from the only adult who ever met the thief and did not transact.

---

## 4.0 ABSORPTION INTO THE THIEVES GUILD AND THE LeBEAU HOUSEHOLD (approx. age 10 onward)

**Jean-Luc LeBeau** — patriarch of the New Orleans Thieves Guild, professional criminal of the old-school honor-code variety, formally adopts the subject. The adoption is, per Jean-Luc's own statements across the Nicieza and Mackie runs, *partly* paternal affection and *largely* prophetic-strategic: there are extant Guild prophecies regarding a child marked by demonic eyes who will end the Guild War. Jean-Luc is buying a piece of the future.

This is critical to the file's understanding of the subject's lifelong relationship to fatherhood and authority. **Jean-Luc is the best father the subject ever had, and Jean-Luc still wanted him primarily for what he could do.** This is the baseline against which every later mentor-figure (Xavier, Sinister, Apocalypse, Magneto-in-Acolyte-frame) is measured. The subject *does not know what an adult man who wants him for no instrumental reason looks like*. The single counter-example (Xavier-with-the-pocket) is so anomalous that it functions in the subject's psyche as a kind of fairy tale rather than a template.

**Henri LeBeau**, Jean-Luc's biological son, becomes the subject's older brother. The relationship is, by all available evidence, warm and uncomplicated — possibly the *least* fraught primary bond in the subject's life. Henri is the model of what a loved child looks like in this household, and the subject watches him for cues, sometimes consciously imitating his ease. (Henri's eventual death, much later, lands on the subject with disproportionate force precisely because Henri represented the only relationship in his early life that had not been transacted.)

**Mercy LeBeau**, Henri's wife, becomes a sister-figure. Subject's adult pattern of *outsized protectiveness toward the wives, sisters, and daughters of his chosen brothers* originates here.

**Tante Mattie** continues as the household's spiritual and healing presence. She is, in the subject's interior life, the closest functional analog to a mother — but she is a *traiteur*, a healer, whose stance toward the child is half-mystical, half-diagnostic. She watches him for signs. The subject grows up being *read* rather than held. This is the single most important characterological consequence of his early household: he learned that adult women look at you to discover what you mean, not who you are. The Rogue selection, decades later, is partly an inversion of this — Rogue, by power, *cannot* read him at touch-range without enormous cost, which makes her, paradoxically, the first adult woman in his life capable of relating to him without scanning him.

**Guild education (approx. ages 10–15).** The formal curriculum, reconstructed from the Nicieza Vol. 1, the *Wedding* tie-ins, and the Vol. 3 flashbacks:

- **Larceny technique.** Sleight, lift, fade, drop, recover.
- **Lockwork.** Pin tumblers, wafer locks, lever locks, basic safe work. Subject reaches journeyman level unusually young.
- **Climbing, rooftop traversal, parkour-equivalent movement.** New Orleans rooftops, balcony work, the architectural vocabulary of the Quarter.
- **Knife work.** Defensive and offensive. The bo-staff specialty emerges later, in the post-exile years.
- **Social engineering.** The Guild's most prized skill and the one in which the subject excels earliest. *Reading the mark, building the cover, sustaining the role, exiting clean.* The subject is identified as a prodigy in this domain by approximately age twelve.
- **The Guild's honor code.** A formal ethical system — what is permitted to be stolen, from whom, under what circumstances, what debts a thief owes another thief, what loyalty looks like under what pressure. The subject internalizes this *deeply*. It is the first systematic moral framework he encounters and it remains, in altered form, the operating moral grammar of his adult life: a relational ethics of loyalty-to-persons over loyalty-to-systems.
- **Mental discipline / shielding.** Less formalized than the rest, but present in the curriculum: the Guild teaches a meditative compartmentalization technique designed to defeat coercion, including (in the Guild's own folklore) telepathic intrusion. This training is the seed of the subject's adult, much-remarked-upon resistance to telepaths — a capacity that becomes consequential in the Sinister, Psylocke, and later Krakoa-era interactions.

**Behavioral pattern formation during this period:**

1. **The split between "what the room sees" and "what the operator does."** Imprinted as professional posture and never relinquished.
2. **Charm as work.** The subject learns, before adolescence, that being charming is a *labor* with a *yield*. He never thereafter mistakes it for spontaneity, even when he allows it to feel spontaneous. (Interior caption material across Nicieza confirms this self-awareness in adulthood.)
3. **Affection and exploitation share a vocabulary.** In the Guild house, the same hand that ruffles your hair tonight will test the contents of your pockets tomorrow as a training exercise. The subject learns that touch is information-gathering and that information-gathering is care. The adult consequences for intimate life are extensive.
4. **Permission to be useful as the price of being kept.** Articulated by the subject only obliquely, but visible in every adult relationship: he earns his seat at every table he sits at, every day, by being more useful than the next operator. He does not, in any documented moment, *rest into* unconditional belonging.

---

## 5.0 THE POWER ONSET (approx. ages 12–14, escalating)

Bio-kinetic charging emerges at or near puberty. The early manifestations are *not* the controlled card-charging of the adult Gambit. They are involuntary, unpredictable, and frightening to the subject and to those around him. Per the Carey and Nicieza retrospectives, early incidents include:

- objects charged accidentally on contact and discharging seconds later;
- escalating yield with emotional load — the more frightened or angry the subject, the larger the kinetic release;
- a documented incident in which a charged object detonates near a fellow Guild trainee, with consequences the subject does not narrate in any documented panel — a silence that, again, the file logs as significant;
- by mid-adolescence, evidence that the power is *escalating faster than his control is developing*, and that left untreated it will eventually destroy him.

**Behavioral consequences of the power onset, in childhood:**

- **The hands become the problem.** The subject's relationship to his own hands changes permanently. He develops the now-iconic *constant small manipulation* — cards, coins, small objects — as both training (controlled minor charges) and self-soothing (the hands occupied are the hands not catastrophic). The card-shuffling tic that defines his adult presentation is, at root, a *trauma management ritual that began in adolescence* and never stopped.
- **Touch becomes negotiated.** Even before Rogue, the subject's adult comfort with restrained, slow, deliberate physical contact — and his discomfort with sudden, unconsidered touch from strangers — traces to this period. He learned in his early teens that uncontrolled touch could kill.
- **The first deep equation.** *To feel strongly is to risk destroying what you touch.* This equation, formed in adolescence as a literal description of his mutant power, becomes in adulthood the unconscious axiom governing all intimate relating. The Sinister surgery later treats the *neural* substrate of the equation. **It does not touch the psychological substrate, which by then is fully formed.**

---

## 6.0 EARLIEST BEHAVIORAL SIGNATURES, CONSOLIDATED

By approximately age fifteen — the threshold of the file's next chronological subsection — the following behavioral patterns are fully laid down and will persist, modified but never abolished, across the next four-plus decades of canon:

- **Hyperthymic public presentation over guilt-saturated interior.** Already present in adolescence per Vol. 3 flashbacks.
- **Charm calibrated as work-product, not spontaneity.**
- **Performative thickening of the Cajun accent with non-Louisianans**, modulated downward with intimates and household members. (Verifiable by comparison of in-household dialogue in Nicieza flashbacks vs. on-mission dialogue in Claremont present-tense.)
- **Hands constantly occupied.** Cards, coins, small charges.
- **Constant low-level environmental scanning.** Exits, pockets, keys, weight of objects, charge-potential.
- **Touching of doorframes and furniture on passage.**
- **Eating fast alone, slowly with company.**
- **Sleep irregularity / preference for late watch.**
- **Strategic excellence + tactical improvisation.** The Guild's signature blend.
- **Mental compartmentalization sufficient to resist intrusion.**
- **Outsized protectiveness of those marked as outsider-within-outsiders.** Origin: his own status, in the Guild household, as the marked child — loved, but never *unmarked*.
- **Acceptance of instrumental love as the natural form of love.** Origin: every primary caregiver.
- **Permission-seeking as ground state.** He has never, in his documented life to this point, occupied a space he was not granted. He has stolen many things. He has never claimed a belonging.

---

## 7.0 SUMMARY FORMULATION OF THE GROUND FLOOR

The subject enters adolescence as a child who has been valued by everyone who raised him and chosen by none of them for himself; whose body is marked in a way that pre-narrates him as cursed in his own community; whose hands are simultaneously his livelihood and the instrument by which he may eventually kill someone he loves; whose moral education is a sophisticated, internally consistent system of *loyalty to persons over loyalty to institutions*; whose earliest formed defenses are charm-as-labor, lying-as-protection, and compartmentalization-as-survival; and whose deepest unverbalized conviction is that **to be permitted to remain near other people, he must be more useful than he is known**.

Every later chapter of this file is, in some structural sense, this child still walking. The Sinister bargain will be offered to a boy who has already learned that powerful men will help you if you give them something. The Morlock guidance will be performed by an operator whose moral grammar has already been trained to obey the man who kept him alive. The Rogue selection will be made by a man whose adolescence taught him that uncontrolled touch destroys. The Antarctica acceptance will be issued by a self that has, since infancy, regarded its presence in any community as conditional rent.

The ground floor is built. The rest of the house follows from it.


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# CHRONOLOGY II — FORMATION & EMERGENCE

## The Adolescent Thief (approx. ages 10–15)

By the time the subject is ten, he is already a working asset of the Thieves Guild rather than a child being trained toward eventual asset status. This distinction is not pedantic. The canon (Nicieza, *Gambit* Vol. 3, #1–4, 1999; Mackie, *Rogue* Vol. 1 retrospective; Layman, *Gambit* Vol. 4, 2004) consistently shows pre-adolescent Remy on jobs — running counter-surveillance, picking locks, slipping into spaces a grown thief cannot — and being paid in approval, lodging, and the right to remain in Jean-Luc's house. There is no preserved record of a period in which he was simply a child within the LeBeau household. He earns his seat at the table from the age at which he can reach it.

The skill curriculum is documentable:

- **Sleight-of-hand**, including the close-magic card work that becomes a lifelong tic (cards shuffled, fanned, palmed, walked across knuckles while idle — depicted in virtually every quiet panel from Claremont onward). The cards are not symbolic affectation; they are the residue of thousands of hours of childhood drill in a guild where palmed objects are the basic unit of labor.
- **Lock and safe work** at the level required to defeat the Guild's own examinations (Jean-Luc's gate-tests, referenced in *Gambit* Vol. 3 #2). The subject is one of the youngest Guild apprentices to pass the third gate.
- **Free climbing and rooftop traversal** of the French Quarter, learned as a child by being smaller than his climbing partners and used as the lead — a body memory that persists into the X-roster, where the subject's preferred infiltration line is always vertical and rooftop-oriented (consistent across Claremont, Lobdell, Carey, Duggan).
- **Social engineering** — the Guild's name for what the adult subject calls reading the room. He is drilled in cold-reading marks, mirroring affect, manufacturing rapport, and disengaging without trace. The flirtation he later deploys at the Xavier Institute is not personality; it is a Guild deliverable, refined since he was a boy.
- **The Guild's mental-discipline curriculum** — meditation, compartmentalization, the structured walling-off of secret information from accessible memory. This is the foundation of his later anomalous resistance to telepathy. He is taught, as a child, that his own mind is a vault with rooms only he may enter, and that this is a moral as well as a tactical posture.
- **The honor code** — the elaborate Guild jurisprudence of debt, *droit*, exile, and obligation. The subject internalizes it absolutely. The adult who later walks willingly into the Antarctica verdict is a man operating not on X-Men ethics but on the Guild ethics he was raised in: *the verdict of the tribunal you have wronged is to be accepted*.

The picking of Charles Xavier's pocket — placed by Claremont in the early 1980s of the in-universe timeline and retold by the subject across multiple runs — occurs in this window. The detail the subject preserves and re-tells, both in flirtation and in self-deprecation, is that **Xavier let him keep the wallet**. This is the first documented encounter in his life with an adult who declined to extract something from him. The subject does not act on it for a decade. But he does not forget it. The Xavier wallet sits in his interior dossier as a marker — *there exists at least one of them who does not require payment* — and the eventual recruitment by the X-Men is, in the subject's private accounting, the cashing of a debt Xavier did not know he was owed.

### The First Power Onsets

The kinetic-charging mutation declares itself in pubertal stages, consistent with mutant-canon norms. The earliest manifestations, recalled in fragmentary flashback (Nicieza, *Gambit* Vol. 3 #14–17; Carey, *X-Men: Legacy* #212 charge-sequence flashbacks), are *involuntary and minor*: objects held too long warm in the palm, a playing card snapped down on a table cracks the wood, a stolen pocket-watch discharges in his coat and burns the lining. The Guild reads these as clumsiness; the subject reads them as evidence that the curse-name (*le diable blanc*) was earned.

Escalation is rapid. By mid-adolescence, the charge is uncontrollable in scale. Whole objects discharge without conscious cue; the threshold drops; the upper bound climbs. The pivotal datum in this phase is **interior** — the subject begins to experience his own body as a weapon he is holding poorly. The psychic-power crisis that begins to surface alongside the kinetic one (the documented latent secondary mutation involving a hypothesized empathic / psi-amplification capacity, which the Sinister intervention also dampens) compounds this: he is receiving more relational data than he can metabolize, and emitting more kinetic energy than he can govern, at the same age. The somatic experience is of being *too much*, in both directions, with no off-switch. Sleep degrades. Appetite collapses in episodes. The first depictions of his chronic insomnia and his anomalous tobacco use date to this period — both, on the evidence, regulatory behaviors recruited at fifteen against a nervous system that has begun firing without permission.

### The Brotherhood of Thieves and Henri

The middle-adolescent years are stabilized, to the degree they are stabilized, by **Henri LeBeau** — Jean-Luc's biological son and the subject's older brother by adoption. Henri is the only member of the Guild whose affection appears, in the canon, to have been *non-instrumental*. He teaches Remy without testing him, includes him without billing him, and is on the documentary record as the first person the subject loves without calculation. Henri's later death (Nicieza, *Gambit* Vol. 3 #4) is one of two losses (the other being Belladonna's exile) whose grief signature is visible in the subject across the rest of the canon: he does not speak Henri's name often, and when he does it is flat and final, in the cadence of a man who has already wept what he is going to weep and has no further negotiating position with the fact.

It is in Henri's company that the subject first encounters the operational reality that *one can be Guild-raised and still good*. This is not a small datum. The adult identity-project — *can a thief be a good man* — has Henri as its first proof of concept and Henri's death as its first refutation.

## The Betrothal and the Duel (approx. ages 17–19)

The arrangement of the marriage to Bella Donna Boudreaux is treaty-instrument first, romance second. The Thieves and Assassins Guilds have been at war for generations; the LeBeau-Boudreaux union is engineered by the patriarchs to seal a peace. The subject is informed of the arrangement, not consulted. Bella Donna is informed of the arrangement, not consulted. They are, by the subject's own retrospection in *X-Men: The Wedding Special* tie-ins and the Mackie *Rogue* mini, **childhood companions** before they are betrothed — they grew up alongside each other in the awareness that they would eventually be each other's spouse, and the affection that develops between them is real, lived-in, and pre-romantic in origin. The eros of the relationship is grafted onto a sibling intimacy. This matters: the subject's first adult love is also a person he cannot fully distinguish from family, and the loss of her recapitulates Henri's loss in advance.

The wedding-day duel with Julien Boudreaux is provoked, in the canon as recorded, by Julien — who refuses the union, attacks the subject, and dies on the subject's blade in a fight the subject does not start but does, decisively, finish. The Guild's law is unambiguous: the killing of a sealed-treaty principal voids the treaty and demands exile. The subject is exiled the same day. Bella Donna is left in the Assassins Guild, now structurally obligated to hunt him; the Boudreaux line is now structurally obligated to hunt him; New Orleans is closed to him at age nineteen.

The behavioral residue of the duel and exile is large and specific:

- The subject **never recasts the duel as self-defense, even when accurately it was**. In every retelling across the canon, he describes it in terms that emphasize his own agency and culpability — "I killed her brother," not "her brother attacked me." This is the earliest documented instance of the lifelong pattern of **claiming guilt in excess of warrant**.
- He leaves without contesting the verdict. He does not appeal to Jean-Luc, does not plead, does not request safe-passage terms. The Antarctica acceptance, fifteen years later, is the second occurrence of a behavior whose template was set on the wedding day.
- He carries the betrothal ring (depicted intermittently in Nicieza and Mackie) for years afterward and does not return it. The first love object becomes the first inaccessible love object. The pattern that climaxes in the Rogue selection — *desire structured around enforced separation* — is laid down here, not at Westchester.
- His relationship to **the Cajun affect** itself shifts post-exile. Within New Orleans the accent was unremarked; outside it, it becomes simultaneously a mark of where he can never return and a tool of charm. He intensifies it deliberately, on the evidence of his own interior monologue in the Nicieza solo, around non-Louisianans — the home he cannot have becomes the costume he wears in the world.

## The Drifting Years (approx. ages 19–22)

Between the exile and the Sinister bargain, the subject is a freelance thief moving through Europe, the Caribbean, and the American coasts. The chronology is poorly preserved by design — both editorially and in-character; the subject does not narrate this period in detail to anyone, and the canon presents it in fragments (the Morocco job, the Tante Mattie correspondence, the brief Genosha-adjacent contracts, the European Guilds work referenced in *Gambit & the X-Ternals* and the Nicieza solo's flashback issues).

What can be established:

- **Power escalation continues**, unmanaged. The subject is now experiencing discharges that endanger civilians. He works alone increasingly because partners are not safe in proximity.
- **He builds a working professional reputation** — among the European thieves' fraternities, among fences, among the small population of mutant-aware criminal operators. This reputation is what makes him locatable by Sinister.
- **He develops the regulatory habits of a man trying to dampen a nervous system in revolt**: the cigarettes become consistent; alcohol use becomes regular though not heavy; sleep schedule degrades further into the working-late-and-taking-watch pattern that persists through the X-Men years; he begins the lifelong habit of long solitary walks at night through whatever city he is in, a habit he never abandons.
- **He returns intermittently to New Orleans in violation of the exile**, masked or by night, to see Tante Mattie and (covertly) Henri. The transgressions are small, performed at risk, and important to him out of proportion to their content. The pattern — *the prohibited city he cannot stop visiting, the family he is not allowed to belong to but cannot abandon* — anticipates exactly the adult relationship with the X-Men post-Antarctica.

## The Sinister Bargain (approx. age 22)

This is the central event of the formation period and the founding wound of the adult psychology. The clinical workup has established its trauma significance; the chronology must establish its mechanics.

The subject's power, by this stage, is killing him. The kinetic discharges are no longer fully voluntary at scale; the latent secondary psi component is producing migraine-equivalents, perceptual flooding, and episodes of dissociation. He is approached — there is no canon detail on the broker, though Tante Mattie's involvement is hinted in Nicieza's run and may or may not be load-bearing — by an agent of Nathaniel Essex, who offers a single transaction: surgical intervention to dampen the power, in exchange for a contracted service to be specified later.

The subject agrees. He is twenty-two. He is dying. He has been alone for three years. The man offering the bargain presents as a clinician, a peer in seriousness, a person who *understands what he is*. The seduction of being understood by a man who has the technical competence to fix him is — for an attachment-disorganized young adult conditioned to be valued instrumentally — irresistible. **He does not negotiate the terms of the future service.** This is operationally important and behaviorally diagnostic: he does not ask what he will be asked to do.

The procedure is performed. The brain tissue removed is, per the consistent retrospective canon, the neural substrate governing both the kinetic charge ceiling and the psi-empathic component. He emerges with power *controllable for the first time in his adult life*. The somatic relief is, on the evidence of his own much-later interior captions, the most profound physical experience of his life to date — and he associates it forever afterward with Sinister. This association is the predictive datum that the clinical workup flagged: *offers of control over his own power from credible figures will always carry, for this subject, the somatic memory of relief*. He is structurally susceptible to the bargain a third time because the first time it produced not damage but **deliverance**.

The cost is delivered in two phases.

**Phase one** is the recruitment and assembly of the Marauders. The subject is instructed to find, vet, and bring together a specific roster of mercenary mutants on Sinister's specifications. He does this. He is good at it — Guild-trained in exactly this skill. He believes, at this stage, that he is assembling a strike team for an operation whose specifics will be revealed at execution. The subject is, in this phase, a fixer. He does not know he is staffing a massacre.

**Phase two** is the Morlock Massacre itself (the in-universe event later canonized in *Uncanny X-Men* #210–213, with the subject's involvement retroactively established in *Uncanny X-Men* #350, Lobdell, 1997). The subject is brought to the tunnels as guide. The specifics of what he was told beforehand vary by retelling; the consistent core is that he understood it as a *targeted* operation, not a slaughter of an entire underground community, and that he did not understand the scale until he was inside it. By that time the Marauders he had assembled were executing in coordinated fashion and there was no instrument by which he could stop them.

He stops them in the only way he can. He breaks contract and saves one child — Sarah, the future Marrow — extracting her from the tunnels at risk to himself. **The single rescue is the seed of the entire adult reaction-formation: the man who organized the massacre will spend the rest of his life pulling the marginal and the marked out of the fire, one at a time, because the first time he did it it was the only morally available act in a corridor of slaughter.** The pattern is laid down by the rescue, not by the crime; the crime is the negative space the rescues are drawn against.

He leaves the tunnels. He does not return to Sinister for further contracts. The bargain is, in his understanding, paid; in Sinister's understanding, opened. The subject will spend the next decade flinching at the edges of his own peripheral vision for the man who built him.

### Immediate Post-Massacre Period

This is the darkest phase of the formation chronology and the least documented in canon, by design — the subject does not narrate it to anyone, and what is preserved is preserved in fragments and inference.

What can be established from behavior:

- The subject **does not turn himself in**, but **does not flee Sinister's reach efficiently either**. He drifts. He works low. He takes small jobs. He sleeps badly and drinks more than he did before. The chronic anhedonia masked by hyperthymic performance — the adult baseline — is acquired in this window.
- He develops the **codename Gambit** in this period (the documented adoption is in Claremont, but the etymology and the act of self-renaming belong here, immediately post-massacre). The name is diagnostic: a gambit is a *sacrificed piece offered for positional advantage*. He has named himself after the sacrifice and the calculation that produced it. The codename functions, from the moment of its adoption, as the **container for the post-crime identity** — *Remy* is the boy from New Orleans who killed Julien, *Gambit* is the man who survived the tunnels. The splitting that the personality section identifies as a primary defense is operationalized in the act of naming.
- He **does not seek out a moral community**. He is not, at this stage, looking for the X-Men or any equivalent. He is, on the evidentiary curve, **waiting** — without conscious plan — for an event that will either kill him or rescue him. He is not suicidal in the active sense; he is *passively available to consequence*, in the same posture he will later assume on the Antarctic ice.

## The Storm Encounter and the Pivot Toward the X-Men (approx. age 25–26)

The recruitment-entry into the X-Men, in *Uncanny X-Men* #266 (Claremont, 1990), is the closing event of the formation period. The behavioral analysis has flagged the diagnostic significance of the choice of Storm — depowered, de-aged, amnesiac, vulnerable — as his entry point. The chronological point to establish is the sequencing.

He encounters the child-Storm by accident, in New Orleans, in the orbit of a thieves' job that has gone wrong. He could leave her. He does not. He **protects her, partners with her, restores her to her allies**, and — through her — gains entry to Westchester. The selection of Storm as broker is not consciously calculated by the subject; the calculation is preconscious, structural, Guild-trained. A vulnerable powerful woman who owes him a debt is, in the Guild's social-engineering grammar, the highest-probability vector into a closed institution. He executes the vector without articulating it.

But the choice of the *X-Men specifically*, when other vectors were available — when he could have used the Storm debt to obtain money, position, leverage, freedom from Sinister — is the formative pivot. He chooses the institution that contains the survivors of the people he helped kill. This is the first overt act of the lifelong penance and the first move in the adult identity-project. He does not tell them what he did. He hopes — and this is the load-bearing word — that *being among them, useful to them, protective of them, daily and without disclosure*, will constitute a form of payment that does not require him to be condemned in order to be made.

It will not. But that is the next chronology's problem. The formation closes here: a twenty-something man with a surgically-modified brain, a New Orleans accent he wears as a costume, a codename that means *the sacrificed piece*, a saved child somewhere in the tunnels he hopes is alive, an exiled fiancée in a Guild contracted to kill him, and a wallet-shaped memory of the one adult who once let him keep what he had taken — walking into Westchester with his hands visible, his charm forward, and the entire load-bearing truth of his life unspoken behind his teeth.


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# CHRONOLOGY III — THE DEFINING MIDDLE PERIOD

*Operational window: from formal integration into the X-Men (c. Uncanny X-Men #266, 1990) through the dissolution of the team into the post-M-Day diaspora (c. 2005-2007). This is the longest continuous documented stretch of the subject's life in stable institutional affiliation, and the period during which every behavioral pattern catalogued in the pre-synthesis file either crystallized, was tested under load, or was demonstrated to be repetition-resistant.*

---

## I. The Probationary Years (1990 – c. 1992)

### I.1 — Entry Vector and Pre-Positioning

The subject's insertion into the X-Men is not, on the evidence, opportunistic. Reviewing the chronology backwards from his integration: he is in the Tier Sur / New Orleans circuit working freelance jobs after the Guild exile, he is in the orbit of brokers and fences who knew the X-Men existed as employers-of-last-resort, and he is in pre-existing covert contact with at least one Sinister cut-out (this is the cleanest read of why Sinister later treats him with the proprietary tone of a *deployed* asset, not a freed one). The subject's encounter with the de-aged Storm in the Morlock tunnels' upper grates is therefore — at minimum — a meeting he allowed to happen and probably one he positioned for.

The behavioral tell is the *immediacy* of his protective alignment with a stranger. He does not interrogate, vet, or extract her — he adopts her. A subject with his Guild training does not adopt strangers; he runs them. The exception is when the stranger is a *broker target*: a person whose protection will purchase him entry to the institution standing behind her. The subject knew, or rapidly inferred, that the small Black child with white hair and weather-affect was a route into Xavier's house.

This is not cynical. It is layered. He genuinely cares about her; he also uses her. Both are true simultaneously and the subject experiences no contradiction between them — a diagnostic in itself.

### I.2 — Establishing the Performance

The first eighteen months on the team are an extended audition during which the subject calibrates the persona that will become the public Gambit. The components observed and refined in this window:

- **The accent management.** Comparison of his dialogue in close, low-stakes scenes with teammates (Storm in the gardens, Jubilee in the kitchen) against his dialogue in front of guests, civilians, and antagonists shows a measurable thickening of the Cajun register in the latter. He is consciously dialing it. The accent is real; the *display* of it is choreographed.
- **The card business.** The playing card is selected as signature in this window. The choice is operationally questionable (it announces him at distance, leaves physical residue, and is unnecessary — anything pocket-sized would charge), and is therefore a *branding* decision. He is building a recognizable silhouette for the team's enemies precisely so that he, individually, becomes the salient threat and his teammates become harder to predict. The cards also serve a sleight function: they explain the hand movement that would otherwise be obviously thievish.
- **The coat.** Adopted within the first arcs and never voluntarily abandoned for the duration of this period. It is too long for tactical work, too warm for most environments, and too distinctive for infiltration — and yet it persists. Function: it carries pocket inventory (lockpicks, the bo staff segments, decks, cash in three currencies, ID forgeries, lighter, cigarettes, a folded knife not Guild-issue), and it is a *silhouette signature* that he can shed in two seconds to become unrecognizable. The coat is a costume worn over a thief.
- **The bo staff.** The weapon is from his Guild training, but his X-Men-era display of it is louder than his Guild-era discipline would warrant. He twirls. The twirling is a performance of nonchalance under fire that re-cues teammates to read him as in-control, even when his interior captions in this window are documenting active fear.

By the end of this probationary window the persona is set: charming, lethal, untrustworthy in petty things and rock-solid in the moment of crisis. The team accepts the persona. None of the team — *including* the resident telepath — penetrates it.

### I.3 — Wolverine, Storm, Xavier: the Triangulated Alliances

The subject's pattern in this window is to seek, in order, three specific bonds:

**Storm** as elder-sister and broker. She vouches for him at the threshold; he repays her with a loyalty that is documentably the most stable in his entire X-Men career. He never lies to Storm in the deep register. He withholds, but he does not deceive. This is the *one* permitted clean channel — the relationship he keeps un-poisoned, presumably because she is the one whose retraction of vouching would unmake him institutionally.

**Wolverine** as peer-with-a-worse-record. The subject reads Wolverine instantly in the first weeks as a man also carrying perpetration trauma, and seeks his presence preferentially in moments of operational tension. The relationship is not warm in the conventional sense — they bait each other, contest each other's deception in professional registers — but the *seeking* is unmistakable. The subject needs an example of a man who has done worse and is still in the room, and Wolverine provides that example by existing.

**Xavier** as the audit he is daring himself to fail. The subject's behavior around Xavier in this window is conspicuously stiff — courteous, even formal, the accent thinning, the flirtation off. He is being good. He is also testing whether the telepath will reach for the buried material. Xavier, per his ethical commitments, does not. The subject reads this as either (a) confirmation that Xavier is moral, or (b) confirmation that his shielding works — both readings reinforce the persona's survival.

### I.4 — Rogue: Selection and Architecture

The pursuit of Rogue begins in this window and is the single most important behavioral evidence in the entire file. The pre-synthesis assessment correctly identifies the structural-impossibility logic. The early-window granular detail:

- He does not approach her first as a romantic target. He approaches her as the *most isolated person on the team* — the one whose powers exile her socially in a manner he recognizes from his own life — and his initial overtures are pitched as solidarity rather than seduction.
- The transition from solidarity-overture to romantic-overture happens with calculated patience over months. He waits until she has come to trust the solidarity, *then* introduces the romantic register, *then* withdraws by a calibrated degree, *then* re-introduces it. This is courtship as Guild infiltration: rapport, ask, retreat, return.
- The *content* of his flirtation is almost never sexually specific. It is endearment-based ("chere," "ma belle"), longing-based, hand-touching-glove-based. The flirtation is structured around a wished-for-but-impossible contact, which is precisely the register in which his avoidance is *invisible*. Were she touchable, his flirtation would have to be sexually specific or expose itself as theatre. Her untouchability protects the theatre's plausibility.

He has, by the end of the probationary years, selected the woman to whom he will give the next decade-plus of his life on the precise condition that she cannot make him give her the truth.

---

## II. The Long Engagement (c. 1992 – 1997): The Five-Year Holding Pattern

### II.1 — Operational Tempo

The middle-middle period is, externally, the subject at his most professionally productive and most domestically stable. He runs missions, holds the southern roster (with Rogue, Bishop, sometimes Beast) together as its social glue, accumulates a working knowledge of the X-Men's logistical seams that exceeds Cyclops's, and becomes — quietly, without announcement — the team member most often deployed for human-intelligence work that requires the team's plausible deniability.

The Genosha incursions, the Muir Isle aftermath, the Mojo intrusions, the post-X-Tinction Agenda reconstruction, the Bishop integration, the Magneto-Asteroid-M crises, the early Acolyte engagements — across each, the subject's contribution is the same operational signature: he is the one who *got in first*, *talked to the wrong person*, *learned the thing the team needed*, and *did not write it in the after-action report*. He is the team's off-book operator, and his after-action discipline is to brief Storm and Cyclops verbally with the strategic inputs while suppressing the tradecraft. The team comes to rely on this without ever interrogating why.

### II.2 — The X-Cutioner's Song Sequence

A critical inflection in this window. During the X-Cutioner's Song arc the subject is subjected to a memory-extraction sequence (in custodial conditions) and the buried Sinister material *almost surfaces*. The subject's response, documented in his interior captions across the immediate post-arc issues, is not relief at near-disclosure but acute terror. He spends the following two to three months noticeably more guarded, drinks more in solitary settings, sleeps in shorter cycles, and conspicuously avoids being alone in a room with Jean Grey.

This is a forecasting failure on his part that costs him later: he treats the near-miss as confirmation that his shielding holds rather than as the warning that the material is structurally retrievable by anyone with the right tool. He does not use the window to make voluntary disclosure to Rogue. The chance, in retrospect, was the cleanest he would ever get. He passes on it. The passing is *consistent* with the avoidant strategy — voluntary disclosure is precisely what the strategy forbids — but it is the central tragic decision of this window and arguably of his life.

### II.3 — Bella Donna Re-Entry

The recurrent Bella Donna arcs in this window function as managed-dose exposure to his pre-X-Men identity. He handles each Bella Donna incursion the same way: he goes south, he handles it personally, he returns minimally explanatory, he closes the file. He does not bring Rogue south for any of these except under duress.

The granular tell: he is *protecting Rogue from the New Orleans persona*, not protecting himself from the Rogue persona. He does not want her to meet the Remy who existed before Gambit, because she has fallen in love with the Gambit, and the older Remy is harder to love.

### II.4 — The Antarctica Bunker Foreshadow

By 1996 he is, on the textual evidence, beginning to crack under the secrecy load. The interior captions become more guilt-saturated. He takes more solo shifts. He volunteers for the morally-grey jobs (the Externals contact, the Black Womb-adjacent leads, the early Onslaught-period running) at a rate that exceeds operational need.

Read carefully, this is **provocation behavior**: the subject is, at the edges of his own awareness, *trying to surface the material* by accumulating present-tense behavior that will require explanation. He cannot voluntarily confess; he can only manufacture conditions in which confession becomes structurally inevitable. He is, in effect, walking toward Antarctica before Antarctica exists.

---

## III. The Antarctica Rupture (1997)

### III.1 — The Trial

The Trial of Gambit sequence is the structuring event of the middle period and the moment around which everything before and after must be reread. The published facts are well-rehearsed in the pre-synthesis. The behavioral granularity:

- **He does not flee.** He is given multiple windows in which a Guild-trained thief of his caliber could have evaporated, and he uses none of them. He attends the trial in the posture of a man who has been waiting for it.
- **He does not mount a defense.** He does not invoke the brain surgery, the power-crisis context, the rescue of Sarah, the duress of the Sinister bargain. He has *contextual mitigation in volume* and deploys none of it. This is not because he forgets; it is because deploying it would constitute *defending himself from the verdict he is paying to receive*.
- **He looks at Rogue, not at the tribunal.** The trial is a private transaction between the two of them, mediated by an audience. The verdict he is seeking is hers; the rest are witnesses.
- **He accepts the abandonment with the affect of relief.** The interior captions in the immediate post-verdict frames document a quieting, not a panic. The terror has been the *anticipation* of disclosure; the disclosure itself is, in the strict sense, a release.

### III.2 — The Cold

The Antarctic survival period is one of the few documented stretches in his life of involuntary solitude under physical extremity. The known behavioral record from his subsequent recountings:

- He stops performing. There is no audience, and the performance lapses. The accent thins in his own interior monologue. The flirtation circuitry powers down. What remains is a quieter, more clipped, more procedural self that the X-Men have essentially never met.
- He does not, on the evidence, plan revenge. He plans only survival, and survival as a kind of duty owed to a future in which he might be useful again. He is not angry at Rogue. He is, on the textual evidence, *grateful* to her for the verdict.
- He encounters the New Sun, Marrow's pre-Marauder echoes, and at least one Sinister cut-out in the post-cold recovery period; he survives all of them; he returns with the post-trial self reinforced rather than dismantled.

The Antarctica period is the closest the subject ever comes to being the unperformed man. He emerges and immediately re-armors. The brief unperformed self is filed and not deployed again.

### III.3 — The Return

The post-Antarctica re-integration is the most behaviorally diagnostic stretch of the middle period. He returns to the X-Men *quietly*, performs no large gesture of reconciliation, asks for nothing, and resumes operational utility at the previous level within weeks. He does not bring up the trial. He does not ask Rogue to take him back. He waits to be re-included or not, on the institution's terms.

This is the behavioral signature of a man for whom acceptance-as-punishment was the *correct* and *sufficient* outcome and for whom restoration is a bonus he does not feel entitled to negotiate for. The team, partially because he asks for nothing, gives him most of what he would have asked for. Rogue does not, in this immediate window, and he does not press.

---

## IV. The Post-Trial Reconstruction (1997 – 2000)

### IV.1 — The Solo-Series Window (Nicieza Run)

The Nicieza solo series functions as a documented diary of the subject's post-trial self-reconstruction. The captioned interiority across this run is the densest psychological record we have of the man at any point in his life.

Key behavioral observations from this window:

- **Active confrontation with the New Orleans past.** He returns south, engages the Guilds on their own terms, mediates Bella Donna, handles the Tithe-Collector and the Externals-adjacent backlog. He is, for the first time, *processing* rather than suppressing the pre-X-Men material. The processing is not therapeutic in any clean sense — he resolves none of the underlying patterns — but he stops sealing the New Orleans self away from the Gambit self for these months.
- **The Tante Mattie consultations.** He returns to the maternal figure he can tolerate and uses her as the closest analog to a therapist he will ever have. He talks. He does not, even with her, disclose the full Morlock material — but he disclosures around its edges, and her counsel is the only counsel he will accept in this period.
- **The introduction of Fiona / the New Sun resolution.** The arc with the New Son (his alternate-future self, lethal, unconstrained) is functionally a confrontation with what he would have been without Sinister's surgery — and, more pointedly, without the X-Men's holding environment. He kills the New Sun and reads the killing as confirmation that the X-Men chose well in keeping him, which steadies him.

### IV.2 — Quiet Rapprochement with Rogue

The post-trial relationship with Rogue does not re-engage as a romance; it re-engages as a slow restoration of trust-in-presence. They work missions together; they share watch; they do not flirt. The flirtation will return later, but in this window it is *off*, and its absence is itself the most honest interpersonal communication of his life.

By the end of the Nicieza run window the romance is restored in modified form: less rakish, more weighted, more capable of sustained silence between them. The relationship has been pruned. It is closer to real now than it was in the long engagement, *because* it has metabolized one rupture.

### IV.3 — The Magneto / Acolytes Inflection

Through the late-90s and turn-of-millennium arcs the subject is repeatedly placed in proximity to Magneto's organization and to figures who offer him alternative father-houses. Across these contacts his behavior is uncharacteristically *cold*. He does not flirt with Magneto's ideology. He does not bait the Acolytes. He treats Magneto as a discrete operational threat and disengages without monologue.

The diagnostic: he has *learned* the susceptibility. The Sinister bargain, recently re-examined in solo-series interiority, has been file-marked as the catastrophic failure mode. He polices himself around any figure whose silhouette matches Sinister's — and Magneto's silhouette matches enough to trigger the policing. He will fail this policing later (Apocalypse will reach him through a different vector — possession rather than offer), but in the Magneto register the policing holds.

---

## V. The Eve-of-Apocalypse Years (2000 – 2003)

### V.1 — Institutional Drift

The early-2000s X-Men editorial environment is fractured: Morrison's New X-Men reorganizes the institution toward a school-centric model in which Gambit does not naturally fit, Claremont's X-Treme X-Men siphons him into an off-book strike team under Storm, Austen's Uncanny run inherits him in a destabilized configuration. The subject's behavioral response to institutional drift is *attaching harder to Storm*. He goes where she goes. The X-Treme tenure is, for him, a regression to the entry-vector relationship: Storm vouches, he serves, the rest is noise.

In this window he is also visibly bored, in the operational sense. The school model does not need an off-book infiltrator daily. He compensates by taking on Guild-adjacent freelance under team auspices and by deepening the relationship with Rogue, who is also somewhat institutionally displaced in this configuration.

### V.2 — The Touch Problem, Iterated

Across these years there are repeated arcs in which Rogue's powers are temporarily, partially, or conditionally modulated — a power-loss arc, a control-acquisition arc, a brief touch-window. The subject's behavior across these is *highly* diagnostic.

He does not, on the evidence, *use* the touch windows the way a man uncomplicatedly hungry for them would use them. He is tender, he is grateful, he is present — and he is also visibly anxious in the interior captions. The structural impossibility that protected him is provisionally lifted, and his system is not configured for the new regime. He loves her; he wants her; and the prospect of her becoming touchable destabilizes the entire architecture of how he has loved her.

He does not sabotage these windows directly. He does experience each closure of them with something the captions can only fairly read as *partial relief*.

### V.3 — The Pre-Possession Vulnerability

By 2003 the subject is, on the cumulative evidence, operating at lower psychological reserve than at any prior point in the middle period. The factors stacking:

- The touch-problem oscillations are exhausting him in ways he will not articulate.
- The institutional drift has reduced his sense of *useful-utility*, the rent he believes he is paying.
- The Sinister material is, after the Nicieza-window partial-processing, *more accessible to him consciously* than it was during the long engagement — which is salutary in principle but corrosive in the daily.
- Bella Donna and the Guild arcs of this period drag him repeatedly south, fragmenting his attention.

He is, in short, *available* for capture in a way he was not available during the long engagement. Apocalypse's operational planners — if we read them as competent — pick the correct moment.

---

## VI. The Horseman Period (c. 2004 – 2008)

### VI.1 — Conversion

The Apocalypse-conversion sequence is the pre-synthesis file's designated repetition of the founding crime, and the granular behavioral evidence supports the reading exhaustively.

- The conversion is not, in the cleanest reading, *purely* coercive. The textual evidence shows resistance — but it also shows a measurable interval in which the subject *accepts* the offered control over his power, even knowing the cost. The interval is brief. It is also real. The Sinister bargain pattern reactivates under the right pressure with the right offer, exactly as the pre-synthesis predicts.
- The Death persona that emerges is *not* unrecognizable. It is the un-policed Remy — the post-Guild, pre-X-Men Remy who never bonded to a moral community — given operational license. The Cajun affect is suppressed; the planner is sharpened; the empathy is gated, not absent. The teammates who encounter Death-Gambit register him as *cold* rather than as *other*. This is diagnostic: the Death state is not possession-by-an-alien-personality, it is *amplification-of-a-suppressed-self*.
- He nearly kills, and notably does not kill, several teammates during this window. The failures-to-kill are the residue of the X-Men attachment surviving inside the conversion. The attachment is not strong enough to refuse the conversion; it is strong enough to corrupt its execution.

### VI.2 — Return and Concealment

Restoration from the Death state is gradual, partial, and incomplete in ways he conceals from the team. He resumes the Gambit persona with visible effort. The sclera color modulations are not entirely reversed. There is, per the Carey run interiority, residual conditioning that surfaces under stress for an extended period.

He does not disclose the residual. He resumes flirtation with Rogue at a slightly reduced intensity. He resumes the off-book operator role for the team. He pretends wholeness — exactly the failure mode the pre-synthesis flags.

The repetition compulsion observation in the pre-synthesis is here vindicated in its strongest form: the *post-conversion* behavior is *identical in shape* to the *post-Sinister* behavior. He has done the same thing twice. He has rationalized it the same way. He has handled the aftermath with the same concealment-and-utility strategy. Twenty-plus years of intervening growth have not modified the underlying response set when the right pressure-and-offer combination is applied.

### VI.3 — Carey Run: The Quiet Stabilization

Through the Carey-era X-Men runs the subject settles into the most operationally mature version of himself observed in the middle period. He partners with Rogue in a quasi-detective configuration on a small mobile team. He stops performing for the wider X-roster because the small-team format does not require it. The Cajun affect modulates downward into something closer to baseline. He carries the post-Death residue with grim competence rather than denial.

Critically, in this window: **he begins, for the first time, to tolerate being known by Rogue without engineering disclosure-by-violation.** The pattern does not break — he is not voluntarily confessing the unconfessed material — but the everyday register of withholding-by-charm relaxes. They have long silences. They have working partnerships in which the performance is off. The relationship is, in the technical sense, *intimate* for the first time, in ways that do not require touch.

This is the closest the middle period comes to a positive prognostic indicator. It will be tested, and largely held, into the late period.

---

## VII. Cumulative Pattern Evolution Across the Middle Period

Stepping back from the arc-by-arc


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# CHRONOLOGY IV — RECENT HISTORY & CURRENT STATE

*File segment covers the period from the dissolution of the Krakoan national project through the present operational window. Cross-reference: Duggan, *Marauders* Vol. 2 (2022–23); Thompson/Rosenberg, *Rogue & Gambit* mini (2023); Orlando, *Rogue: The Savage Land* (2024); Percy/MacKay X-line interstitials; Ewing, *X-Men* relaunch threads; *Fall of X* / *Rise of the Powers of X* terminal sequences; and ambient cross-title appearances.*

---

## 1. THE LATE KRAKOAN PERIOD: CAPTAINCY AND DOMESTICITY

The subject's terminal Krakoan-era posting was as field captain of the Marauders — first under Kate Pryde, then in the relaunched Marauders configuration that took the *Marauder* (vessel) into deep-space and inter-temporal action. The symbolic restitution noted in §1 of the workup — the man who once led murderers under that name now leading rescuers under that name — was not, in the subject's own private operational code, *symbolism*. It was payment. Files of his interior captioning across the Duggan run show a consistent semantic pairing: "the team" and the verb *owe*. He did not lead the Marauders; he *owed* them. The captaincy was a posting he sought, not one he accepted.
